It was a weed, already sprouting in the corner of the herb bed, green and vigorous and demanding to be dealt with.

Tomorrow.



---

# Chapter 24

## **Chapter 24**

The man was trying to live, but the paperwork was a nightmare. Ariston, the former shade, had his head in his hands, elbows planted on a rough-hewn table, squinting at a clay tablet as if it were written in serpent’s tongue.

“It says here,” Daphne murmured, her finger tracing the incised lines, her voice soft with the morning and a lingering disbelief, “that to claim title to a plot of land previously designated as ‘Elysian-Adjacent Contested Ground,’ we need a seal from the local magistrate, two witnesses of good standing, and a sworn affidavit that the resident dryad, if any, has voluntarily vacated.”

“Voluntarily vacated?” Ariston groaned. He was clothed, save for a tunic that kept snagging on the scab over his sternum. His body was a map of fading bruises and new calluses. “The ‘resident dryad’ was you. And you didn’t vacate. You… rooted.”

“A technicality,” she said, a ghost of her old, leafy stubbornness in her smile. She pushed the tablet aside. “We could just live here. Who would know?”

“The tax assessor,” Ariston said darkly. “He’ll know. They always know. It’s a sixth sense. They can smell unregistered olive trees from three valleys away.”

They were in the courtyard of the small, crumbling farmhouse that stood on the land. Their land, if they could ever prove it. The house had come with the earth, a forgotten shell of sun-baked brick and sagging timber. It smelled of dust, old rosemary, and possibility. The garden where they had woken—truly woken—sprawled beyond the low wall, a tangle of lavender, defiant weeds, and that single, buried acorn.

The hook from yesterday—the vigorous weed in the herb bed—had been pulled. It now lay wilted on a compost heap. A hundred new hooks had taken its place: a leaking roof, a missing amphora, the profound and mundane question of what to eat for lunch when you are responsible for acquiring the lunch yourself.

It was glorious.

It was exhausting.

Charon, had he been present, would have appreciated the irony. The bureaucracy of the afterlife had been frustrating, but it was a system. You waited your turn, you filled your forms, you were processed. The bureaucracy of life was messier. It was magistrates with bad tempers and good days. It was rain that didn’t fall on the schedule you’d hoped for. It was a body that got hungry at inconvenient times and tired before the work was done.

Ariston had died because a form wasn’t filed. Now, he couldn’t legally live because of another.

“Forget the tablet,” Daphne said, standing. She stretched, and the movement was purely, wonderfully human—a ripple of muscle under sun-warmed skin, a crack in a joint, a sigh of contentment. “The sun is high. The fig needs pruning. The bureaucracy can wait.”

They worked. It was different now. Before, in the half-life of the myth, their labor had been a kind of haunting, a repetitive echo of a memory. Now, it was negotiation. The soil resisted the hoe. A thorn snagged Ariston’s thumb, and a bead of blood, shockingly red and immediate, welled up. He stared at it, not with fear, but with a profound sense of occasion. *My blood. Here. Now.* He sucked the wound, the taste of iron and dirt a sacrament.

Daphne wrestled with a stubborn vine. She wasn’t a nymph commanding the green; she was a woman arguing with it. She won some battles, lost others. Sweat traced a clean path through the dust on her temple. Once, she paused, her hand on the rough bark of their old fig tree. She closed her eyes, not to listen, but simply to feel. The sun on her lids. The grit of bark. The faint, remembered scent of sap, now just a memory, not a conversation.

“It’s quiet,” she said, not opening her eyes.

“Is it?” Ariston leaned on his hoe. He heard bees. The distant, complaining bleat of a goat from a neighboring farm. The wind in the olives.

“In here,” she said, touching her chest, then her temple. “The… audience is gone. The story is over. It’s just the noise we make.”

It was the truest thing. The myth had been a shout in a canyon, full of echoes and import. This was the sound of their own breath, their own tools, their own occasional, quiet words. It was a smaller sound. A real one.

As afternoon softened into evening, a shadow fell across the garden gate. It was not the looming, bureaucratic shadow of a god or a ferryman. It was the compact, worried shadow of a man in a slightly-too-nice chiton, clutching a waxed slate.

“Hail,” the man said, his voice trying for authority and landing somewhere near anxious officiousness. “I am looking for the… occupants? Of this plot. Regarding the matter of unverified tenancy and possible cultivation of a demarked divine precinct.”

Ariston and Daphne exchanged a look. This was it. The first tax assessor.

“We’re the occupants,” Ariston said, wiping his hands on his tunic. He didn’t bother trying to look respectable. He looked like what he was: a man who’d been digging.

The assessor, whose name was Philo, made a note on his slate. “And your lineage? Your claim?”

“The claim is sweat,” Daphne said, coming to stand beside Ariston. “And blood.” She took Ariston’s pricked thumb and held it up for Philo to see, the tiny scab like a badge.

Philo blinked. “That’s… not a recognized form of deed.”

“What is?” Ariston asked.

“Well, inheritance. Purchase. Grant from a recognized deity or ruling body.” Philo peered past them at the farmhouse, the garden. “This land was noted as… fallow. Possibly cursed. There was an incident with a previous owner and a… botanical obsession.”

“The curse is lifted,” Daphne said simply. “The obsession has passed.”

Philo looked at them. He saw two people who were too old to be starting a farm, yet who moved with a strange, earned solidity. He saw the woman’s hands, nails dark with soil, and the man’s chest, where the fabric of his tune dipped to show a remarkable, star-shaped bruise fading to yellow and green. He saw no slaves, no livestock save a few chickens scratching in the dust, no sign of wealth but the terrifying vitality of the untamed garden.

“You understand,” Philo said, lowering his slate slightly, his bureaucratic demeanor giving way to a flicker of human curiosity, “without a deed, the city could claim this land. Auction it. You’d be tenants, at best.”

“We understand,” Ariston said. He felt no panic. The threat was earthly, manageable. “What would it take? To make the deed.”

“A witness of standing,” Philo said, ticking it off. “The magistrate’s seal. And a history. You can’t just be people who appeared from the dirt.”

“But we did,” Daphne said, and her smile was not argumentative, but conspiratorial. “Perhaps you could be our witness, Philo. You’ve seen us. We are here. You could write that down on your slate. ‘I, Philo, assessor of the Eleusinian district, did on this day see two people, very much alive, tending this land. They seemed… determined.’”

Philo stared at her. He was a small man with a small job, used to being ignored or lied to. He was not used to being offered a role in a story. He looked at the garden again, at the carefully pruned fig, at the neat(ish) rows among the chaos, at the simple, solid fact of their presence.

“That’s… not procedure,” he said weakly.

“What is procedure,” Ariston asked, “but the story everyone has agreed to tell? You could start a new one.”

Philo was silent for a long moment. The bees hummed. A chicken clucked. He looked from his official slate to their expectant, unafraid faces. He sighed, a sound that seemed to deflate his own importance.

“I will put down ‘squatters with improvement potential,’” he said, scribbling. “It’s a category. It buys you time. A season, perhaps. The magistrate… well, he likes figs. Really good ones.”

He left soon after, his step less officious, a single, perfect fig from their tree clutched in his hand—a bribe that was not a bribe, but a sample of potential.

That night, they ate a simple meal of bread, oil, and herbs from the garden on the roof of the farmhouse. The stars were vast, cold, and indifferent. They were no longer points of divine intrigue or celestial fate. They were just stars. Beautiful in their distance.

“Do you miss it?” Ariston asked, his voice quiet in the immense dark. “The power? The… connection?”

Daphne considered. She thought of the deep, silent knowing of the earth, the flow of seasons in her veins. She thought of the laurel’s whispered vanity, the oak’s stubbornness.

“I miss the certainty,” she said finally. “Knowing what I was. Root and branch. Now…” She held up her hand, silhouetted against the Milky Way. “Now I am this. And it is fragile. It aches at the end of the day. It will age. It will forget things. It is a story I am telling myself in the dark, and I am not sure of the ending.”

Ariston took her hand. The warmth was a fact. The pulse under his thumb was a rhythm. “Neither am I.”

“That’s the gift,” she said, turning to him. In the starlight, her eyes were dark pools, but alive with reflected light. “And the theft. We don’t know the ending. We only know the next line.”

He kissed her then, and it was not a mythical kiss, not a symbol of anything. It was salty from the olives, a little clumsy with fatigue, and real. It was a choice made in the dark, under the indifferent stars, by two people who had nothing but the moment they were in.

The days unspooled, each one a ledger of small gains and losses. They repaired the roof. The first shoots of wheat they planted were eaten by birds. Daphne wept with frustration, real, hot, human tears that left tracks in the dust on her cheeks. Ariston held her, saying nothing, because there was nothing to say that the birds would care about.

They learned their neighbors. There was old Myrrine, who kept the goats and brought them cheese, her eyes sharp with the curiosity of the perpetually lonely. She asked no questions about their past, only offered advice on pruning vines and warnings about the man who sold donkeys at the market. There was Lysander, a young, sun-burned farmer with a laugh like a donkey himself, who helped Ariston lift a fallen beam in exchange for help mending a wall.

They were not characters in a drama to these people. They were just the odd couple in the old cursed plot. It was a kind of anonymity more profound than any they had known.

One afternoon, a storm blew in from the sea. It was not a divine tempest, sent to test them. It was a spring squall, full of practical malice. They rushed to secure the chickens, to prop up the young bean plants with sticks. The wind tore a section of their newly-repaired roof away. The rain poured in, a cold, drenching invasion.

They stood in their main room, ankle-deep in chilling water, watching their meager possessions float. A clay cup. A spare tunic. The wretched clay tablet with the land laws.

Ariston began to laugh.

It was a raw, wild sound, torn from the pit of his stomach. Daphne looked at him as if he’d gone mad. Then she saw it—the absolute, ridiculous, catastrophic *normalcy* of it. This was life. It wasn’t a swan from heaven. It was a leaky roof. It was a problem they could, eventually, solve. She joined him, her laughter mixing with his, a mad duet against the drumming rain.

They bailed out the water with cracked amphorae. They spent the night huddled by a sputtering fire, wrapped in damp blankets, sharing body heat. It was, Ariston thought, more intimate than any Elysian field. It was need, met by another’s warmth. It was a shared defense against a common, ordinary cold.

The next morning, the world was washed clean. The air sparkled. And in the corner of the herb bed, where the first weed had been, a green shoot pushed through the mud. Not a weed. A slender, fragile stem with two tiny, perfect leaves.

The acorn.

It had survived the deluge. It had chosen, in its silent, vegetable way, to begin.

Daphne knelt beside it, not in worship, but in wonder. She did not touch it. She simply observed. It was so small. So easily crushed by a misplaced foot, devoured by a bug, withered by the next day’s sun. Its survival was not a promise, but a chance. A bet placed against the universe.

“Look,” she whispered.

Ariston crouched beside her. He saw the seedling, a defiant speck of green in the brown mud. He saw the reflection of it in Daphne’s eyes. He saw the ruined roof, the water-stained walls, the endless work waiting for them.

It was enough. It was everything.

The season turned. The seedling grew, slowly. They acquired a dog, a skinny, grateful creature with one ear chewed off, who appeared at their gate and never left. They named him Bureaucrat, for his persistence and his fondness for sleeping on important documents.

Philo the assessor returned, ate another fig, and told them the magistrate was “considering” their case. It was neither good nor bad news. It was a postponement. A stay of execution. A lifetime, if they were lucky.

One evening, as the heat of summer began to soften, Ariston found Daphne standing at the edge of their property, looking down the road that wound towards the city, the river, the world.

“What do you see?” he asked, coming to stand beside her.

“A road,” she said. “It goes somewhere else. We could walk down it. We could go to the city. See a play. Buy a pot that isn’t cracked.” She leaned into him. “We don’t have to.”

“No,” he agreed. “We don’t.”

The freedom of it was dizzying. They were not bound to this land by a god’s decree or a metamorphic curse. They were bound by choice. By the tender shoot of the oak. By the ghost of a myth they had outlived. It was a thinner thread, but a stronger one, because they held both ends.

That night, Ariston had a dream. He was back on the grey shore, the mist coiling around his ankles. Charon’s skiff was there, but it was empty, beached on the silt. The ferryman himself was sitting on a rock, his robe hiked up, dipping his bony feet in the water. He was not polishing a coin. He was skipping stones.

“You,” Charon said, without looking up. His voice was still gravel, but the gravel was dry now, baking in a non-existent sun. “I should have known. The ones who make a fuss about the paperwork are always the ones who sneak back.”

“I didn’t sneak,” Ariston said. “I was… recalled.”

“Semantics.” Charon sent a flat, grey stone hopping twice across the still water. “It makes a mess of my ledgers. ‘Ariston: Cause of Death – Swan. Status – Deceased.’ Now I have to add an asterisk. ‘*Provisional. Subject to review.’ I hate asterisks.”

“I’m sorry for the inconvenience.”

Charon finally looked at him. The hollow sockets were not frightening, just tired. “Are you? You’re living. It’s the ultimate inconvenience. To everyone. Especially to yourself.” He threw another stone. It sank immediately. “Is it worth it? The leaking roofs? The taxes? The… *weeding*?”

Ariston thought of the cold night by the fire, Daphne’s head on his shoulder. The taste of the first tomato from their garden, warm from the sun. The ridiculous, wagging tail of the dog they called Bureaucrat. The tiny oak leaf, unfurling.

“Yes,” he said.

Charon nodded, as if he’d expected the answer, as if he’d heard it before in a thousand different tones. “Good. Then stop visiting me in your dreams. I have enough to do.”

The dream dissolved into the sensation of a warm body beside him, and the smell of sun on skin.

The final test, when it came, was not from the gods, or the underworld, or the magistrate.

It was from the past.

A traveler appeared on the road. A man in a fine, but dusty, cloak. He had a familiar, handsome face, now lined with years and what looked like too much wine. It was Leon, Ariston’s oldest friend. The one who had, in another life, comforted Daphne in her garden while her husband was being murdered by a waterfowl.

He stopped at their gate, his expression one of bewildered recognition. “By all the gods… Ariston? They said you were dead. They said… a swan?”

Ariston, who was mending a fishing net, looked up. The world seemed to slow. Daphne emerged from the house, a clay jar in her hands. She froze.

The three of them stood, a triangle of shared and separate histories. Leon’s eyes darted from Ariston’s face to the fading, but still visible, mark on his chest, to Daphne, who was so clearly, vibrantly *there*.

“I was,” Ariston said carefully. “I got better.”

Leon laughed, a nervous, disbelieving bark. “A jest. A cruel one. I mourned you. I… I was there for Daphne.” His eyes sought hers, pleading for confirmation of a story he understood.

Daphne set the jar down. She walked to the gate, not to open it, but to stand beside Ariston. She looked at Leon, not with old anger or old grief, but with a calm, clear pity. “You were,” she said. “And then I left. To find him.”

“But the story…” Leon sputtered. “The oracle said… you became a tree. A laurel. It’s famous!”

“Stories end,” Ariston said. His voice was quiet, but firm. It was the voice of a man who had argued with a ferryman and won. “Or they change. This is our story now. It’s a small one. About a roof, and an oak tree, and a dog.”

Leon stared at them, his mouth slightly open. He was a man who lived on stories, on gossip, on the grand narratives of heroes and nymphs and transformations. He did not know what to do with a story about mending nets and planting acorns. He saw the calluses, the simple clothes, the quiet solidarity between them. He saw a truth that was smaller, and therefore infinitely larger, than any myth.

“I see,” he said, though he clearly didn’t. He took a step back. “Well. I am… glad. For your health.” The words were hollow. He had come hoping for a tale to tell, a brush with the legendary. He was being offered a cup of water and a seat in the shade, which was not the same thing at all.

He declined the water. He turned and walked back down the road, his shoulders slumped, a man who had encountered a miracle and found it mundane.

Ariston and Daphne watched him go.

“He’ll talk,” Daphne said.

“Let him,” Ariston replied. “They’ll think it’s a metaphor. Or a lie. Or that he had too much sun. They won’t believe him.” He took her hand. “The truth is too plain.”

The magistrate’s decision arrived on the last day of summer. Philo brought it, looking uncharacteristically solemn. He held a sealed scroll.

“It’s the ruling,” he said.

Ariston broke the seal. He unrolled the papyrus. He read it aloud, his voice steady.

“*By the authority of the Magistrate of Eleusis, the plot designated as the former Grove of Daphne is hereby granted in freehold to the persons known as Ariston and Daphne, cultivators of said land. Their claim, witnessed by Philo the Assessor and supported by evidence of sustained improvement, is recognized. The past designation of the land is struck from the record. It is henceforth to be known as…*” He paused, a smile touching his lips. “*The Oak-Fig Farm.*”

There were no trumpets. No celestial signs. Philo accepted a cup of wine. The dog barked. Daphne let out a breath she felt she’d been holding for centuries.

They were legal. They were real. They existed in the records of men.

That night, they celebrated with old Myrrine and Lysander. They ate, they drank rough wine, they laughed. Lysander played a tuneless song on a flute. Myrrine told a long, rambling story about a goat who fell in love with a statue. It was ordinary. It was perfect.

Later, when the guests had stumbled home and the moon was high, Ariston and Daphne walked to the garden. The oak seedling was now a foot tall, a brave little spear of life in the quiet dark. They stood before it, hand in hand.

They had come full circle, but it was not the circle of a noose or a closed loop. It was the circle of a spiral. They were back in a garden, but they were not the same. He was not a man trying to die. She was not a nymph trying to escape. They were two people, trying to live. The bureaucracy of eternity had been traded for the bureaucracy of the seasons, of the body, of the heart. It was a better deal.

The lens through which they had stumbled—love, mortality—found its ultimate expression not in a grand passion or a heroic death, but in this: the shared, daily acknowledgment of the limit. The knowledge that this—the feel of a calloused palm, the sound of a familiar breath in the dark, the slow growth of a tree they would not see mature—was borrowed. Precious because it would be taken. Beautiful because it was brief.

They had no answers. They had no guarantees. They had a deed to a farm, a scar on his chest, soil permanently under her nails, and a finite number of sunsets ahead, each one a gift, and a theft.

It was enough. It was everything.

The hook for the next day was not a weed, or a tax man, or a ghost from the past.

It was the first autumn chill on the air, a sharp, clean note promising apples, and woodsmoke, and the slow, golden decline.

Tomorrow.
